            if (typeof (gptText) == "string") {
                gptText = gptText.replace(/\&/g, '%26');
                return gptText.replace(/\^|\*|#|\$|\"|\'|\=|\,|\!|\+|\~|\;|\(|\)|\<|\>|\[|\]|\?|\%|\:|\@|\;|\&|\||\{|\}|\\|/g, '');
            }
            return "blank";
        }

            .setTargeting('property', 'value-of-property')    // Change and duplicate this line for as many additional keywords to send
            .setTargeting('doi', gptClean('10.1002/adma.202311523')) // Publication DOI
            .setTargeting('pbPage', gptClean(gptPage))
            .setTargeting('pbSubPage', gptClean(gptSubPage))
            .setTargeting('urlpath', gptClean(document.location.pathname));

            .defineSizeMapping(sizes.leaderboard)

    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="d427df3c9c424740bf7cfac4f3e691ca">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '1b2f1473-32d2-4cbf-8fc2-5c6cd1638450'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d155113011" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            
            <p>Layered transition-metal (TM) oxide cathodes have attracted growing attention in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs). However, their practical implementation is plagued by Jahn–Teller distortion and irreversible cation migration, leading to severe voltage decay and structure instability. Herein, O3-Na<sub>0.898</sub>K<sub>0.058</sub>Ni<sub>0.396</sub>Fe<sub>0.098</sub>Mn<sub>0.396</sub>Ti<sub>0.092</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (KT-NFM) is reported as an ultrastable cathode material via multisite substitution with rigid KO<sub>6</sub> pillars and flexible TiO<sub>6</sub> octahedra. The K pillars induce contracted TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs and their strong Coulombic repulsion to inhibit Ni/Fe migration; and Ti incorporation reinforces the hybridization of Ni(3deg*)-O(2p) to mitigate the undesired O3–O'3 phase transition. These enable the reversible redox of Ni<sub>2</sub>+↔Ni<sub>3</sub><sub>.</sub><sub>20</sub>+ and Fe<sub>3</sub>+↔Fe<sub>3.69</sub>+ for 138.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and ultrastable cycles with &gt;90% capacity retention after 2000 cycles in a pouch cell of KT-NFM||hard carbon. This will provide insights into the design of ultrastable layered cathode materials of sodium-ion batteries and beyond.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202311523-sec-0010">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202311523-sec-0010-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Sodium takes significant advantages over lithium due to its abundance and worldwide availability, ensuring sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) with economic sustainability in large-scale energy storage devices.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0001_R_d155112736e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1 - Nat. Rev. Mater.">1</a>-<a href="#adma202311523-bib-0003" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0003_R_d155112736e469" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 3 - Chem. Rev.">3</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Na-based layered transition metal oxides Na<i><sub>x</sub></i>TMO<sub>2</sub> hold promise for their high specific capacity and scalable synthesis.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0004_R_d155112736e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Adv. Mater.">4</a>-<a href="#adma202311523-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0007_R_d155112736e479" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Energy Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Its lattices are built up by alternating transition-metal oxide slabs (TMO<sub>2</sub>) and sodium oxide (NaO<sub>2</sub>) slabs, where TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs determine the chemical/electrochemical properties and NaO<sub>2</sub> slabs provide insertion site of Na<sup>+</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0008_R_d155112736e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Chem. Mater.">8</a>-<a href="#adma202311523-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0011_R_d155112736e496" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - J. Phys. C: Solid State Phys.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> However, the strong O–O electrostatic repulsion upon charge/discharge induces anisotropic lattice displacements and gliding of TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs, and further leads to complex phase transitions and electrochemical deterioration. Simultaneously, the large-sized Na<sup>+</sup> yields Na<sup>+</sup>/vacancy rearrangement in NaO<sub>2</sub> slabs due to the Na–Na repulsion, and results in sluggish diffusion kinetics. This necessitates modulation of TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs and NaO<sub>2</sub> slabs with appropriate compositions and local structures for high-performance SIBs.</p>
         
         <p>O3-type nickel-based oxide cathodes have gained extensive interests because of their multiple electron redox reactions and suitable potentials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0012_R_d155112736e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - A. Chem.">12</a>-<a href="#adma202311523-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0015_R_d155112736e517" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Nevertheless, they suffer from Jahn–Teller distortion of NiO<sub>6</sub> octahedra, and it is induced by the single-electron filled eg (dx2-y2, dz2) orbitals of high-spin Ni<sub>3</sub>+ (t<sub>2</sub>g<sub>3</sub>-eg<sub>1</sub>) and inevitably leads to TMO<sub>2</sub> slab gliding, anisotropic strain propagation and aggravating chemo-mechanical degradation.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0016_R_d155112736e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Nature">16</a>, <a href="#adma202311523-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0017_R_d155112736e535" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Nat. Energy">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> On the other hand, cation migration is widely observed at high voltages in layered transition-metal oxides, which prompts the cumulative formation of spinel-like phases and serious voltage decay upon cycling.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0018_R_d155112736e541" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Chem. Mater.">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Cation substitution with Li+ or/and Co<sub>3</sub>+ has been attempted to regulate their migration from the TM to Na layers and prevent the movement of Fe and Ni ions to improve the cycling stability. However, they lead to TM vacancies and malposition with metastable state in TM layer, which leads to increased kinetic barrier and structure instabilities.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0019_R_d155112736e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Energy Fuels">19</a>, <a href="#adma202311523-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0020_R_d155112736e548" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - Sci. Bull.">20</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This inspires efforts to modulate the coordination chemistry and local strain of the TMO<sub>2</sub> and NaO<sub>2</sub> slabs for stable layered nickel-based cathode materials.</p>
         
         <p>Herein, an ultrastable O3-Na<sub>0.898</sub>K<sub>0.058</sub>Ni<sub>0.396</sub>Fe<sub>0.098</sub>Mn<sub>0.396</sub>Ti<sub>0.092</sub>O<sub>2</sub> is obtained with incorporation of K and Ti into its respective NaO<sub>2</sub> and TMO<sub>2</sub> layers for rigid KO<sub>6</sub> pillars and flexible TiO<sub>6</sub> octahedra. Their roles in the regulation of the electrochemical performance are systematically studied by operando characterizations and density functional theory calculations. The incorporation of Ti can enhance the Ni(3deg*)-O(2p) hybridization and eliminate the undesired O3–O'3 phase transition; and the K riveted in NaO<sub>2</sub> slabs inhibits Ni/Fe migration through the strong Coulombic repulsion between its two adjacent TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs. These reward reversible capacity of 138.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> and long lifespan with &gt;90% capacity retention after 2000 cycles in KT-NFM||Hard carbon (KT-NFM||HC) pouch cell.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202311523-sec-0020">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202311523-sec-0020-title">2 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202311523-sec-0030">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202311523-sec-0030-title">2.1 Structure and Analysis</h3>
            
            <p>The materials were synthesized via solid-state reactions, which are described in the Supporting Information. Their stoichiometries were determined to be Na<sub>0.898</sub>K<sub>0.058</sub>Ni<sub>0.396</sub>Fe<sub>0.098</sub>Mn<sub>0.396</sub>Ti<sub>0.092</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (KT-NFM), Na<sub>0.987</sub>Ni<sub>0.396</sub>Fe<sub>0.204</sub>Mn<sub>0.402</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (NFM), Na<sub>0.998</sub>Ni<sub>0.402</sub>Fe<sub>0.104</sub>Mn<sub>0.402</sub>Ti<sub>0.096</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (T-NFM), and Na<sub>0.896</sub> K<sub>0.056</sub>Ni<sub>0.399</sub>Fe<sub>0.202</sub>Mn<sub>0.398</sub>O<sub>2</sub> (K-NFM) by inductively coupled plasma-atomic emission spectroscopy (ICP-AES) in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1</a> (Supporting Information). X-ray diffraction (XRD) patterns and their corresponding Rietveld refinements in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0001">1a</a>; and Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S1,S2</a> (Supporting Information) indicate that they belong to a rhombohedral lattice with R<sub>3</sub>m symmetry and have a typical O3-type structure.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0021_R_d155112736e672" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Energy Mater.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The refined lattice parameters and elemental occupancies are listed in Tables <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S2,S3</a> (Supporting Information). Its crystallographic structure along the <i>c</i> axis in the inset of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0001">1a</a> exhibits that K and Ti occupy the 3a and 3b Wyckoff sites,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0022_R_d155112736e686" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - Adv. Funct. Mater.">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> suggesting K and Ti sitting in the Na and TM layers, respectively. The average TM<i>─</i>O and Na<i>─</i>O bond lengths of KT-NFM are fitted to be 2.034 and 2.331 Å, contrasting to 2.082 and 2.283 Å of NFM, indicating shrinkage of TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs and enlargement of TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs in KT-NFM (Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information). Moreover, the O<i>─</i>O interlayer distance is enlarged from 3.457 Å in NFM to 3.603 Å in KT-NFM owing to the larger ionic radius of K+ versus Na+, as indicated in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S3</a> (Supporting Information). It enables the extension of intermediate triangular and tetrahedral space sites of the Na+ transport channel, which is conducive to accelerating sodium diffusion. This synergistic incorporation of K and Ti induces contracted TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs of 2.06 Å and expanded NaO<sub>2</sub> slabs of 3.32 Å in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information), manifesting reinforced TMO<sub>6</sub> octahedra and weakened NaO<sub>6</sub> octahedra interaction. These are further validated by the redshift of Ni and Fe 2p X-ray photoelectron spectroscopy (XPS) and the positive shift of Na 1s XPS spectra in KT-NFM (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a>, Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202311523-fig-0001"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/e70591f4-f167-4063-a5b7-d7db4a4f97cd/adma202311523-fig-0001-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/e70591f4-f167-4063-a5b7-d7db4a4f97cd/adma202311523-fig-0001-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/52bbd1e1-1808-47e3-8393-0ba3d47b229a/adma202311523-fig-0001-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/e70591f4-f167-4063-a5b7-d7db4a4f97cd/adma202311523-fig-0001-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 1<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202311523-fig-0001&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202311523" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) XRD pattern and Rietveld refinement of KT-NFM and its inset crystallographic structure. b) STEM-HAADF and c) STEM-ABF images, d) Intensity line profile, and e) SAED pattern of KT-NFM. f) Solid-state 39K NMR spectra of KT-NFM and KMO. g) XANES spectra for NFM and KT-NFM at the Ni K-edge. h) Ni K-edge EXAFS and fitting and i) DOS of KT-NFM. j) Electronic structure and k) DSC profiles of NFM and KT-NFM.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The atomic occupations in KT-NFM and NFM were investigated by scanning transmission electron microscopy (STEM) equipped with detectors of high-angle annular dark field (HAADF) and annular bright field (ABF) in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0001">1b,c</a>, as well as in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information). The STEM-HAADF image displays that all TMs indicated by the bright dots are located in the TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs. The distance of adjacent TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs is measured to be 5.38 Å in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0001">1d</a>, and the spacing between adjacent TMs is 2.84 Å in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a> (Supporting Information); and Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0001">1c</a> exhibits the STEM-ABF image with alternating TMO<sub>2</sub> and NaO<sub>2</sub> slabs in an ABCABC subsequence, indicating its typical O3-type structure. The selected area electron diffraction (SAED) pattern in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0001">1e</a> corroborates that the as-synthesized KT-NFM is a single crystal. The STEM-HAADF image and its corresponding intensity line profile in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information) indicate that NFM and KT-NFM possess similar stacking subsequences and layer structures.</p>
            
            <p>Solid-state 39 K nuclear magnetic resonance (NMR) spectra of KT-NFM and K<sub>0.67</sub>MnO<sub>2</sub> (KMO) were displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0001">1f</a>. The peak around 0&nbsp;ppm is assigned to K+ in the K layers of KMO, and two symmetrical weak peaks at 70 and −70&nbsp;ppm are spinning sidebands due to rotation of sample tubes.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0023_R_d155112736e785" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Nat. Commun.">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Its negative chemical shift from 0 to −40&nbsp;ppm in KT-NFM is ascribed to the electronic shielding effect of Na and O, and implies the K presence in Na layers.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0024_R_d155112736e791" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Solid State Nucl. Magn. Reson.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Scanning electron microscopy (SEM) images in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a> (Supporting Information) reveal their flake-like morphology with basal plane facets of ≈5&nbsp;µm in width and lateral facets of ≈300&nbsp;nm in thickness. High-resolution transmission electron microscopy (HRTEM) images in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8,S9</a> (Supporting Information) present clear lattice fringes of the (003) plane with interplanar spacings of 5.38 Å in KT-NFM and 5.31 Å in NFM, respectively. The energy-dispersive spectrometry (EDS) mappings display homogeneous distributions of each element without segregation in both NFM and KT-NFM.</p>
            
            <p>X-ray absorption fine structure (XAFS) analyses at the Fe and Ni K-edge were performed to investigate chemical states and local structures of KT-NFM and NFM. Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0001">1g</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information) display X-ray absorption near edge structure (XANES) spectra at the Ni and Fe K-edge in KT-NFM and NFM, where NiO and Fe<sub>2</sub>O<sub>3</sub> spectra are collected as reference. The absorption edge positions of Ni and Fe in KT-NFM are positively shifted from 8349.12 to 8349.56&nbsp;eV and 7032.01 to 7032.32&nbsp;eV, respectively, indicating higher chemical valence of Ni and Fe. In the Fourier-transformed extended X-ray adsorption fine structure (FT-EXAFS) spectra in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0001">1h</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information), two major peaks are attributed to the first coordination of TM<i>─</i>O and second coordination of TM–TM, respectively. The reduction in the average Ni<i>─</i>O and Fe<i>─</i>O bond lengths listed in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S5</a> (Supporting Information) implies the enhanced hybridization of Ni<i>─</i>O and Fe<i>─</i>O bonding after K and Ti incorporation.</p>
            
            <p>The density of states (DOS) of KT-NFM and NFM was obtained via density functional theory (DFT) calculations to study their electronic structures in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0001">1i</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information). The TM(3deg*)-O(2p) hybridization orbitals with a major contribution of TM 3d determine the redox reaction of layered oxides, according to the molecular orbital energy diagram of TMO<sub>6</sub> octahedra and the electronic orbitals of Ni<sub>2</sub>+/Fe<sub>3</sub>+/Mn<sub>4</sub>+ in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S13</a> (Supporting Information).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0025_R_d155112736e858" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Nat. Commun.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is observed that the energy level of Fe(3deg*)-O(2p) orbital is lower than that of Ni(3deg*)-O(2p) orbital near Fermi level (EF) in both KT-NFM and NFM, suggesting that Ni<sub>2</sub>+ is more prone to electron donation and being oxidized compared to Fe<sub>3</sub>+ during charge.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0026_R_d155112736e868" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This is evident with the higher average energy of the occupied Ni(3deg*) orbital (−0.25&nbsp;eV) than that of occupied Fe(3deg*) orbital (−0.52&nbsp;eV) in KT-NFM, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information). Moreover, the overlapped region and energy position of Ni(3deg*)-O(2p) orbital is greatly increased in KT-NFM, implying elevated Ni<i>─</i>O bonding energy. It facilitates to reinforce TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs and suppresses undesirable phase transitions, as presented in the schematic electronic structure in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0001">1j</a>. Differential scanning calorimetry (DSC) was performed to evaluate the thermal stability of KT-NFM and NFM in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0001">1k</a>, which were scratched from electrode pellets charged to 4.0&nbsp;V. KT-NFM presents an exothermal peak at higher temperature of 297.7&nbsp;°C with less heat of 402.5 J g<sup>−1</sup> than those of NFM (274.2&nbsp;°C and 630.8 J g<sup>−1</sup>), indicating its enhanced thermal stability.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202311523-sec-0040">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202311523-sec-0040-title">2.2 Structure Evolution</h3>
            
            <p>In situ XRD was performed to monitor structure evolution of KT-NFM and NFM upon Na+ extraction/intercalation in a home-made battery within 2.0–4.0&nbsp;V. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0002">2a</a> presents the initial charge curve, 2D contour maps and their refined lattice parameters of KT-NFM. When charged from the onset potential to 3.04&nbsp;V, the (003)O3 and (104)O3 peaks consecutively shift to lower and higher angles, respectively, which is induced by the O<i>─</i>O electrostatic repulsion and TM oxidation, and thus leads to expanded c and contracted a lattices.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0027_R_d155112736e908" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Chem. Mater.">27</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon further charged to 3.15&nbsp;V, the emerging (108)P3 at 57.4° is ascribed to the Na<i>─</i>Na repulsion and suggests a two-phase reaction.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0028_R_d155112736e916" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - Chem. Mater.">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> As the charge proceeds, the characteristic O3 peaks disappear and the (108)P3 maintains till 4.0&nbsp;V, implying Na extraction through solid-solution reaction mechanism. The (003)P3 is then positively shifted to (003)O3 without degrading crystallinity in the subsequent discharge process in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S14,S15</a> (Supporting Information), indicating the reversible P3–O3 phase transition in KT-NFM.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202311523-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/77d06264-4df4-4f59-a453-2060a1e5adfe/adma202311523-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/77d06264-4df4-4f59-a453-2060a1e5adfe/adma202311523-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/aa30dcc6-b469-4d1a-b9d6-9277c92a8793/adma202311523-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/77d06264-4df4-4f59-a453-2060a1e5adfe/adma202311523-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202311523-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202311523" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Initial charge curves, contour maps of in situ XRD and corresponding lattice parameter changes of a) KT-NFM and b) NFM. c) COHP analyses of representative octahedra sites of Ni<sub>12</sub>, Fe<sub>2</sub>, and Mn<sub>2</sub> in KT-NFM.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>As a comparison, the (104) peak of O3 phase in NFM splits into (20<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/6eb90444-d100-43a0-9fcd-f7c4dd585acb/adma202311523-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202311523:adma202311523-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202311523-math-0001.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mover accent="true">
                        <mn>2</mn>
                        <mo>¯</mo>
                     </mover>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{2}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>) and (111) during initial charge for a monoclinic O'3 phase with C<sub>2</sub>/m space group.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0029_R_d155112736e963" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Inorg. Chem.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This is due to the increased amount of Jahn–Teller active Ni<sub>3</sub>+ upon Na+ extraction (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0002">2b</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S16</a>, Supporting Information). The elevated degeneracy of the Ni<sub>3</sub>+ 3d orbitals will lead to elongation or shrinkage of axial Ni<i>─</i>O bonds, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S17</a> (Supporting Information). The low-symmetric O'3 phase maintains till 2.87&nbsp;V with ≈0.18 Na+ extracted, as indicated by the disappearance of (001)O'3, (002)O'3, and (13<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/4e5fbc60-be18-41d9-9f18-50ad7956750a/adma202311523-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202311523:adma202311523-math-0002" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202311523-math-0002.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mover accent="true">
                        <mn>3</mn>
                        <mo>¯</mo>
                     </mover>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{3}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>)O'3. This O3–O'3 phase reaction usually involves extra kinetic barriers for nucleation of anisotropic variation and spatial symmetry change, which leads to large interfacial energy and microcracks in electrode materials.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0029_R_d155112736e993" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - Inorg. Chem.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Upon charging to 4.0&nbsp;V, the (006)P3 peak starts to appear and then moves to low angles. These lead to dramatic c lattice expansion and a lattice contraction in NFM (+7.58% and −4.36%), and they are in contrast with those of +3.64% and −1.98% in KT-NFM, indicating the alleviated volume changes and enhanced structural integrity of KT-NFM.</p>
            
            <p>In situ XRD patterns of T-NFM and K-NFM were also collected in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S18–S20</a> (Supporting Information) to illustrate the roles of K and Ti in their structure evolution. The absence of O3–O’3 phase transition in both KT-NFM and T-NFM reveals that Ti substitution functions to suppress long-range concentrated Ni<sub>3</sub>+ Jahn–Teller distortion. This is confirmed by a slight distortion index (DI),<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0030_R_d155112736e1006" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - J. Mater. Chem. A">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> increases (137% and 148%) of NiO<sub>6</sub> octahedra from the pristine to charged state at 4.0&nbsp;V of KT-NFM and T-NFM (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S21</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S6</a>, Supporting Information), which is in sharp contrast to 585% in NFM and 370% in K-NFM. The DI values of TiO<sub>6</sub> octahedra are calculated in a range of ≈0.04–0.05, suggesting their inherent flexibility to mitigate the anisotropic variation of Ni<i>─</i>O bond lengths and enhance structure stability during charge and discharge. For MnO<sub>6</sub> octahedra, it experiences small and steady DI values at different Na contents, which implies that Mn<sub>3</sub>+ Jahn–Teller distortion is not involved during charge and discharge. The suppressed O3–O'3 and delayed O3–P3 phase transition are also observed in both KT-NFM and K-NFM, and it implies that K is riveted as rigid pillars to alleviate the gliding of TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs. It is evidenced by the slight DI increase (7.0% and 6.2%) of KO<sub>6</sub> octahedra from the pristine to charged state at 4.0&nbsp;V in KT-NFM and K-NFM.</p>
            
            <p>Crystal orbital Hamilton population (COHP) analyses were performed by DFT calculations to reveal the impact of multisite substitutions on the TM<i>─</i>O bond energy. The enhanced average integrated COHP of Ni<i>─</i>O, Fe<i>─</i>O, and Mn<i>─</i>O in KT-NFM are visualized in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0002">2c</a>. Their values are determined to be 1.306, 1.124, and 1.058, respectively, implying enhanced TM 3d-O 2p covalency, which is sharply distinct from those of NFM (1.089, 1.077, and 1.045, Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S22</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S7</a>, Supporting Information). Among the different TM<i>─</i>O bonds, Ni<i>─</i>O bond is increased by 20% in the average integrated COHP, which leads to the removal of eg orbital degeneracy and thus suppresses Ni<sub>3</sub>+ Jahn–Teller distortion in KT-NFM. The redox of KT-NFM was unveiled by XPS in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S23</a> (Supporting Information) to be Ni<sub>2</sub>+↔Ni<sub>3.20</sub>+ and Fe<sub>3</sub>+↔Fe<sub>3.69</sub>+ for charge compensation. The signals from Mn 2p3/2 and Mn 2p1/2 at ≈642.1 and 653.3&nbsp;eV, and Ti 2p3/2 and Ti 2p1/2 at ≈458.2 and 464.1&nbsp;eV reveal the existence of Mn<sub>4</sub>+ and Ti<sub>4</sub>+ at different states,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0031_R_d155112736e1077" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Mater.">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> suggesting that they are inactive for redox reaction, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S24</a> (Supporting Information). This is consistent with the hybridization of unhybridized O(2p)NB orbitals with the Mn (3dt2g) orbitals in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            
            <p>STEM-HAADF images of KT-NFM and NFM charged to 4.0&nbsp;V were recorded to visualize their atomic rearrangements. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0003">3a,b</a> displays alternating bright columns with a TM-TM layer distance of 5.7 Å in both surface and bulk regions of KT-NFM, confirming its hexagonal structure. This is further corroborated by the emerged peaks at 3a Wyckoff sites<span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0031_R_d155112736e1097" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Chem. Mater.">31</a></sup></span> in the intensity line profile (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0003">3c</a>) and the characteristic spot of (003) facets in FFT pattern (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0003">3d,e</a>) shows remarkable spots between the neighboring TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs in NFM, and it indicates TM cation migration to Na layers. The disordered spinel-like structure in the sub-surface Zone I and rock-salt structure in the surface Zone II are indicated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0003">3e</a>. They are evidenced by the observed blue and red peaks at 8a and 3b Wyckoff sites in the intensity line profile of NFM, respectively (Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0003">3f,g</a>). The emerging facets of (1<span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/aac8d84e-e1dd-4f5d-8fe5-277c8453544f/adma202311523-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:09359648:media:adma202311523:adma202311523-math-0003" display="inline" location="graphic/adma202311523-math-0003.png">
                  <semantics>
                     <mover accent="true">
                        <mn>1</mn>
                        <mo>¯</mo>
                     </mover>
                     <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">$\bar{1}$</annotation>
                  </semantics></math>1) along [110] in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0003">3h,i</a> correspond to the spinel-like structure with Fd-3m space group and rock-salt structure with Fm-3m space group in the FFT patterns, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0032" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0032_R_d155112736e1128" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 32 - Adv. Energy Mater.">32</a>, <a href="#adma202311523-bib-0033" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0033_R_d155112736e1128" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 33 - eScience">33</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Raman spectra in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S25</a> (Supporting Information) further verify the presence of spinel and rock-salt phases in the charged NFM with two dynamic peaks at 563 and 615 cm<sup>−1</sup>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0034" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0034_R_d155112736e1138" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 34 - Nat. Mater.">34</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> due to cation migration. The STEM-HAADF images and their corresponding intensity line profiles in the bulk regions of the charged KT-NFM and NFM are depicted in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S26,S27</a> (Supporting Information). It exhibits alternative TM columns without popped spots in the charged KT-NFM, in sharp contrast to strong cation migration in the charged NFM.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202311523-fig-0003"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/71a30d7e-641c-4505-a197-13677cd423cd/adma202311523-fig-0003-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/71a30d7e-641c-4505-a197-13677cd423cd/adma202311523-fig-0003-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/25a95371-5b21-4ec7-8c05-5e2d7bd9c705/adma202311523-fig-0003-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/71a30d7e-641c-4505-a197-13677cd423cd/adma202311523-fig-0003-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 3<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202311523-fig-0003&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202311523" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">STEM-HAADF images of a) surface and b) bulk regions of charged KT-NFM. c) Intensity line profile and d) FFT pattern of charged KT-NFM. e) STEM-HAADF image of charged-NFM. f,g) Intensity line profiles of dotted rectangle regions in (e). h,i) FFT patterns of Zone I and Zone II in (e). j,k) Proposed mechanism for inhibition of cation migration.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The possible mechanism in inhibiting interlayer cation migration in KT-NFM is proposed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0003">3j,k</a>. Interlayer cation migration in layered oxides initially occurs via the route of octahedra→tetrahedra with formation of TM occupation of 8a sites and TM vacancies at 3a sites, namely, (TM8a-V3a) antisite defects, as illustrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0003">3j</a>. Upon charging, the cation subsequently migrates from the TM8a to 3b sites in Na layers, forming TM occupancy at the 3a sites and TM vacancies at the 8a sites for the defects (TM3b-V8a).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0035" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0035_R_d155112736e1175" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 35 - Nat. Mater.">35</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This is driven by the percolating networks of empty tetrahedral sites that have no face-sharing occupied octahedral sites.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0036" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0036_R_d155112736e1180" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 36 - Chem. Mater.">36</a>-<a href="#adma202311523-bib-0038" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0038_R_d155112736e1180" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 38 - Angew. Chem., Int. Ed.">38</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> For KT-NFM in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0003">3k</a>, TM ions are anchored by the strong Coulombic repulsion between the NaO<sub>2</sub> slabs with large-sized K+ and TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs, and TM migration from the adjacent 8a to 3b sites is inhibited. It is further studied by DFT calculations with the optimized structures at the pristine and fully charged states of KT-NFM and NFM, as depicted in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S28,S29</a> (Supporting Information), respectively. The contour map of charge density on the (102) plane of KT-NFM (<b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0004">4a</a>) presents higher electron density around O than those of NFM (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S30</a>, Supporting Information), suggesting enhanced TM<i>─</i>O and weakened Na<i>─</i>O covalent bonds, which is consistent with the XPS results in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S4</a> (Supporting Information). It is further confirmed by −1.26e of average charge around O atoms in TiO<sub>6</sub> octahedra in Bader charge analyses (Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S31</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S8</a>, Supporting Information). The strengthened oxygen ligand framework induced by K+ riveted in Na layers favors inhibition of cation migration in KT-NFM.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202311523-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/504b3514-01c7-4f02-a992-40ca1094991d/adma202311523-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/504b3514-01c7-4f02-a992-40ca1094991d/adma202311523-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/e1c6d3cd-d4cc-4efe-a9c9-aa6e5ec18afa/adma202311523-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/504b3514-01c7-4f02-a992-40ca1094991d/adma202311523-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 4<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202311523-fig-0004&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202311523" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) Contour map of charge density on (102) plane of KT-NFM. b) MSDs of Na, K, Ni, Fe, Mn, Ti, and O as functions of time in KT-NFM. TM migration paths from 3a-8a-3b sites in c) NFM and d) KT-NFM. e) Relative site energies of Ni migration in optimized structures of NFM and KT-NFM.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>First-principle molecular dynamics simulations were employed to analyze the mean square displacements (MSDs) of each element of KT-NFM and NFM in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0004">4b</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S32a</a> (Supporting Information). It shows that only Na is mobile and K is anchored in Na layers as pillars. This agrees with the constant K stoichiometry during charge and discharge, as verified by ICP-OES in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S9</a> (Supporting Information). The interlayer cation migration paths in NFM and KT-NFM are proposed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0004">4c,d</a>, respectively. The resultant energy landscapes for both Ni and Fe migration in KT-NFM and NFM are displayed in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0004">4e</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S32b</a> (Supporting Information), respectively. The relative energies of Ni at the 8a and 3b sites in KT-NFM are 1.24 and 3.93&nbsp;eV, respectively, much higher than −3.02 and −2.10&nbsp;eV in NFM, and the similar trend is applicable for the Fe migration in KT-NFM and NFM. These indicate that the interlayer migration of Ni and Fe in KT-NFM is thermodynamically unfavored, due to the strong Coulombic repulsion between two TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs sandwiching K+, which is consistent with the STEM-HAADF images in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0003">3</a>.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202311523-sec-0050">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202311523-sec-0050-title">2.3 Electrochemical Performance</h3>
            
            <p>The electrochemical performance of NFM, K-NFM, T-NFM, and KT-NFM is evaluated against Na metal anodes in coin cells. <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0005">5a</a>; and Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S33</a> (Supporting Information) exhibit the cyclic voltammetry (CV) curves of these electrodes, in which KT-NFM shows a higher couple of redox peaks at 3.02/2.76&nbsp;V. This is because Ti4+ with filled d10 electrons does not participate in the hybridization with O(2p) orbitals to delocalize hole and electrons.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0039" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0039_R_d155112736e1286" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 39 - Small">39</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A much broader CV curve within ≈2.70–3.10 and ≈3.20–3.70 V is observed for KT-NFM, suggesting eliminated O3–O'3 phase transition with large solid-solution region and suppressed Na+/vacancies rearrangement.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0040" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0040_R_d155112736e1292" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 40 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">40</a>, <a href="#adma202311523-bib-0041" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0041_R_d155112736e1292" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 41 - Nat. Commun.">41</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> KT-NFM presents smooth charge/discharge curve with reversible capacity of 138.6 mAh g<sup>−1</sup> at 5&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>, which is slightly smaller than 145.8&nbsp;mAh&nbsp;g<sup>−1</sup> of NFM with multiple voltage plateaus in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0005">5b</a>. This is due to smaller Fe amount in KT-NFM, in line with the CV curves in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0005">5a</a>. Galvanostatic Intermittent Titration Technique (GITT) tests were employed to study Na+ diffusivities in KT-NFM and NFM in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S34,S35</a> (Supporting Information). The calculated diffusion coefficient of Na+ in KT-NFM is between 10<sup>−11</sup> and 10<sup>−10</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup>, which is one order higher than that of NFM. It agrees well with the apparent diffusion coefficient 7.8 × 10<sup>−11</sup> cm<sup>2</sup> s<sup>−1</sup> from CV curves at different sweeping rates in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S36</a> (Supporting Information). In addition, the b values for the major oxidation/reduction peaks in KT-NFM are higher than those in NFM Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S37</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S10</a> (Supporting Information) to favor the fast-redox kinetics and superior rate performance.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0042" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0042_R_d155112736e1338" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 42 - J. Am. Chem. Soc.">42</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This guarantees higher rate capacity retention of 83.8% in KT-NFM than 59.7% in NFM at 500&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> against 10&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0005">5c</a>.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202311523-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/71cd215e-dc27-42fd-8f4c-26740d1d19cc/adma202311523-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/71cd215e-dc27-42fd-8f4c-26740d1d19cc/adma202311523-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b2b59cdc-f4f0-4059-aa20-e6b93b7d4478/adma202311523-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/71cd215e-dc27-42fd-8f4c-26740d1d19cc/adma202311523-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202311523-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202311523" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">a) CV profiles of the second cycle at 0.1&nbsp;mV s<sup>−1</sup>. b) Charge/discharge curves between 2.0 and 4.0&nbsp;V at 5&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. c) Rate capability at varied rates of KT-NFM and NFM. d) Cycling performance of KT-NFM||HC and NFM||HC pouch cells at 200&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. e) Charge/discharge curves of KT-NFM||HC pouch cell in selected cycles.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>The cycling performance of NFM, K-NFM, T-NFM, and KT-NFM are performed in coin cells against Na anodes at 200&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup>. In Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S38</a> (Supporting Information), KT-NFM shows capacity retention of 92.8% after 500 cycles, much higher than NFM, K-NFM, and T-NFM (30.2%, 66.5%, and 60.2%). Pouch cells were constructed with cathode material of KT-NFM or NFM and hard carbon (HC) within ≈1.9–3.9&nbsp;V at 200&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0005">5d</a>. KT-NFM||HC with capacity of 0.6 Ah presents excellent retention of 90.3% after 2000 cycles, with capacity loss of ≈0.005% per cycle. It is contrasting to the degrading capacity retention of 29.63% of NFM. The charge/discharge profiles of KT-NFM||HC in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0005">5e</a> are almost overlapped in the selected 1st, 10th, and 2000th cycles, in which negligible discharge voltage decay of 0.05&nbsp;mV cycle<sup>−1</sup> is presented during 2000 cycles in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S39</a> (Supporting Information), to imply its high reversibility and stability. These are further confirmed by slightly increased charge transfer resistance from 46.9 to 58.1 Ω after 100 cycles in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S40</a> and Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S11</a> (Supporting Information), indicating that the cumulative formation of spinel-like and rock-salt phases are inhibited by the strong Coulombic repulsion between K and TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs. KT-NFM||HC with 135.6&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> is demonstrated to show good capacity retention after 100 cycles at 20&nbsp;mA g<sup>−1</sup> in Figures <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S41,S42</a> (Supporting Information). The remarkable performance of KT-NFM can be attributed to its structure stability and facile Na+ transport.</p>
            </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__sub-content" id="adma202311523-sec-0060">
            
            <h3 class="article-section__sub-title section2" id="adma202311523-sec-0060-title">2.4 Structure and Air Stability</h3>
            
            <p>The structure stability of KT-NFM and NFM was investigated by ex situ SEM and STEM-HAADF after treated with focused ion beam-scanning electron microscopy (FIB-SEM) after 100 cycles. The SEM images at different magnifications in <b>Figure</b>&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0006">6a,b</a> display severe delamination damages with imbricated morphology in cycled NFM. This is induced by the gliding of TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs and anisotropic strain propagation, as verified by a massive of intergranular and intragranular cracks in the FIB-SEM image of Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0006">6c</a>. The cracks expose the particles to the electrolyte and exacerbate side reactions and thus result in particle pulverization and performance degradation of NFM.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0043" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0043_R_d155112736e1434" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 43 - Adv. Mater.">43</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> A different situation is observed in the cycled KT-NFM to show reserved plate-like morphologies and crack-free cross-sections in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0006">6d–f</a>. In a close view, severe Na/TM mixing rock-salt structure and lattice voids are observed in Regions I and II in the HAADF-STEM images of cycled NFM in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0006">6g–i</a>, suggesting TM migration and loss during cycling. This is quantitatively confirmed by ICP-OES, in which Ni, Fe, and Mn concentrations deposited on Na anodes in the cycled Na//NFM are 3100, 460, and 320 ppb, respectively, in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S43</a> (Supporting Information). The K in KT-NFM after 50 cycles was revealed by ICP-OES and XPS analysis to show no change after 50 cycles in Table <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S12</a> (Supporting Information), suggesting its high stability. This agrees well with no characteristic peaks in K 1s region from XPS spectrum in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S44</a> (Supporting Information). The cycled KT-NFM shows a slight bending of the TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs near the surface and no inferior rock-salt phases in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0006">6j</a>. The structure stability of KT-NFM guarantees its ultrastable electrochemical performance, as demonstrated in Figure&nbsp;<a href="#adma202311523-fig-0005">5</a>.</p>
            
            <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
               <figure class="figure" id="adma202311523-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/753db2ab-2f3f-4e2b-8fbe-92c1cae5b1e3/adma202311523-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                        <source srcset="/cms/asset/753db2ab-2f3f-4e2b-8fbe-92c1cae5b1e3/adma202311523-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/10f5f2a2-97f8-4270-8a51-3926832be628/adma202311523-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/753db2ab-2f3f-4e2b-8fbe-92c1cae5b1e3/adma202311523-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                     <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adma202311523-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadma.202311523" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                     </div>
                     <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">SEM images and FIB-SEM images of a,b,c) cycled NFM and d,e,f) cycled KT-NFM. g) STEM-HAADF image and its enlarged h) Region I and i) Region II of cycled NFM. j) STEM-HAADF image of cycled KT-NFM.</div>
                  </figcaption>
               </figure>
            </section>
            <p>Fourier transform infrared spectroscopy (FTIR) and XRD were applied to evaluate the air stability of KT-NFM and NFM. The FTIR spectra of both air-exposed and argon-protected KT-NFM are overlapped without any impurity signals in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S45</a> (Supporting Information), and this agrees well with the XRD patterns in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S46</a> (Supporting Information). However, the air-exposed NFM displays two characteristic peaks at 1430 and 870 cm<sup>−1</sup> of Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, and Na<sub>2</sub>CO<sub>3</sub>, and NaHCO<sub>3</sub>∙H<sub>2</sub>O were detected in both the air-exposed NFM for 24 h and 7 days in the XRD patterns.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adma202311523-bib-0044" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adma202311523-bib-0044_R_d155112736e1507" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 44 - Adv. Mater.">44</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This ensures almost overlapped charge and discharge curves and good cycling performance of KT-NFM stored at different conditions, as shown in Figure <a class="suppLink scrollableLink" href="#support-information-section">S47</a> (Supporting Information).</p>
            </section>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adma202311523-sec-0070">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202311523-sec-0070-title">3 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>In summary, a multisite substitution strategy is applied to synthesize ultrastable KT-NFM with K and Ti in the Na and TMO<sub>2</sub> layers, respectively. It is revealed that the K pillars function to reinforce the TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs and alleviate their gliding. The strong Coulombic repulsion between the adjacent TMO<sub>2</sub> slabs sandwiching K inhibits migration of Ni or Fe. The Ti incorporation favors the Ni(3deg*)-O(2p) hybridization and mitigates Jahn–Teller distortion of NiO<sub>6</sub> to eliminate the inferior phase transition of O3–O'3. Their combination enables the structure stability without cracks and TM migration and loss in KT-NFM. It is demonstrated to deliver high energy density of 135.6&nbsp;Wh kg<sup>−1</sup> and rate capability, and show ultrastable cycling life with capacity retention &gt;90% after 2000 cycles and negligible voltage decay in pouch cells of KT-NFM||HC. The multisite substitution strategy for regulation of coordination chemistry will provide new insights into the reaction mechanism and materials design of SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adma202311523-sec-0080-title"> Acknowledgements</h2>
         
         <p>This work was supported by the National Natural Science Foundation of China (Nos. 22325902 and 52171215) and Haihe Laboratory of Sustainable Chemical Transformations. This research used resources of the Analysis Platform of New Matter Structure at Nankai University.</p>
         
         <ol></ol>
      </div>
